import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a transposed 3D convolution operation with asymmetric input and a square kernel.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the square convolution kernel.
        stride (int or tuple, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int or tuple, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        output_padding (int or tuple, optional): Additional size added to one side of each dimension in the output shape. 
                                                  Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int or tuple, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, output_padding: int = 0, 
                 dilation: int = 1, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose3d = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, (kernel_size, kernel_size, kernel_size), 
                                                stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding, 
                                                dilation=dilation, groups=groups, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the transposed 3D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, depth_out, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose3d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 48
out_channels = 24
kernel_size = 3
depth = 96
height = 96
width = 96

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size]  # Provide in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size for initialization

So it's a full 3D transposed convolution with kernel 3, stride 1, padding 0, no output padding. That is essentially a regular 3D convolution but with transposed semantics (i.e., computing gradient w.r.t. input using conv3d weight? Wait ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to a dense convolution: output = conv_transpose3d(input, weight, bias). In fact, for stride=1 and padding=0, there is no upsampling; conv transpose reduces to cross-correlation with the weight rotated 180 degrees? Actually conv_transpose3d with stride=1 and padding=0, output_padding=0 is equivalent to a conv3d with the weight flipped along all spatial dimensions? Let's recall: In 2D, conv_transpose2d with stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to a regular conv2d with flipped kernel and padding=kernel_size-1 if you consider the transpose of conv2d. But if no padding and stride=1, the output shape of conv_transpose2d is (N, C_out, H + K -1, W + K -1). Wait conv2d with stride=1, padding=0 reduces size by K-1. The transpose of that operation should increase size by K-1. Yes, conv_transpose3d with stride=1, padding=0 gives output shape: D_out = D + (kernel_size-1)*dilation (since stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0). For D=96, K=3, dilation=1 => D_out = 98. So output is 8 x 24 x 98 x 98 x 98. That is bigger than input. This is essentially a "full" convolution: output = conv_full3d(input, weight) where weight is rotated? Actually the gradient of conv3d(wrt input) with stride=1, padding=0 is a full convolution with the weight (not flipped?) But in PyTorch, ConvTranspose3d computes something akin to conv_transpose = conv3d with zero padding K-1 and using the same weight but maybe reversed? When stride=1, conv_transpose3d is equivalent to conv3d with padding=K-1, but using the weight transposed? Wait groups=1. Let's derive: Consider conv3d: output[b, o, z, y, x] = sum_i sum_k sum_l sum_m weight[o, i, k, l, m] * input[b, i, z+k, y+l, x+m] (with appropriate strides/padding). For stride=1, padding=0, output size = D - K + 1. The transpose operation (adjoint) w.r.t. input: given grad_output of shape (N, O, D-K+1, H-K+1, W-K+1), grad_input = conv_transpose3d(grad_output, weight) yields shape (N, I, D, H, W). Specifically, grad_input[b, i, z, y, x] = sum_o sum_k sum_l sum_m weight[o, i, k, l, m] * grad_output[b, o, z - k, y - l, x - m] (with appropriate boundaries). This is exactly a full convolution of grad_output with weight (where weight is not flipped, because it's correlation). But note the indices: grad_output index is z - k, so as k runs 0..K-1, the grad_output is shifted opposite direction relative to weight. This is effectively a convolution (not cross-correlation) with weight. In cross-correlation the index is +k. So indeed it's a convolution with flipped weight. But PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d implementation uses the weight as stored, and the forward operation is equivalent to conv3d of the input padded with K-1 zeros on each side and using weight rotated 180 degrees (i.e., weight[o, i, K-1-k, K-1-l, K-1-m]). Wait, is that how PyTorch implements it? Actually the PyTorch documentation: ConvTranspose3d applies a 3D transposed convolution, which is equivalent to conv3d with padding=K-1 and using the same weight but with spatial dimensions reversed? Let's check from memory: In PyTorch, ConvTranspose2d is implemented using im2col + gemm, or more generally calls cudnn. It does not rotate weights, but the mathematical operation is the transpose of conv2d, which corresponds to cross-correlation with rotated weights. The weight tensor is stored as-is; the algorithm computes the same effect without physically rotating. So essentially forward of ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to conv3d(kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=2) with weight rotated 180 degrees? Wait no: For stride=1, padding=0 conv_transpose, output size = input_size + kernel_size - 1. That is the same as conv3d with padding=kernel_size - 1, stride=1 applied to the input, but using weight flipped. Actually standard conv3d with padding=2, stride=1 on input of size 96 yields output size = 96 + 2*2 - 3 + 1 = 96 + 2 = 98. Yes same shape. So conv_transpose3d = conv3d(padding=K-1) with flipped weight. However, because the reference will compute via ConvTranspose3d, we can implement it as a direct convolution using a custom CUDA kernel. But does PyTorch's conv3d (with flipped weight) produce numerically identical output to conv_transpose3d? Let's test mentally for small inputs. Consider 1D: conv1d with stride=1, padding=0: output[i] = sum_j w[j] * input[i+j]. ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0: output[i] = sum_j w[j] * input[i-j] (where input index valid). That's exactly convolution (not cross-correlation). conv1d with padding=K-1: output[i] = sum_j w[j] * input[i+j - (K-1)]? Wait conv1d with padding=K-1 has input padded with K-1 zeros on each side. Then output index i corresponds to centering the kernel at position i in padded input. The padded input index is i + j - (K-1)? Let's derive: conv1d cross-correlation: output[i] = sum_j w[j] * input_padded[i + j] where i runs 0..N+K-2. If padding=K-1 on left, then input_padded[t] = input[t-(K-1)] for t in [K-1, K-1+N-1]. So output[i] = sum_j w[j] * input[i + j - (K-1)] = sum_j w[j] * input[i - (K-1-j)] = sum_j w[K-1-j] * input[i - j]. If we define w_rotated[j] = w[K-1-j], then output[i] = sum_j w_rotated[j] * input[i - j], which matches conv_transpose1d. Good. So conv_transpose = conv with padding=K-1 and rotated weight. But writing a custom conv3d kernel that is faster than PyTorch's CUDNN conv_transpose3d is very challenging because CUDNN is highly optimized. However, maybe we can exploit the fact that kernel_size=3, groups=1, in_channels=48, out_channels=24, batch=8, spatial 96^3. The weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels, K, K, K) for transpose? Here in_channels=48, out_channels=24, groups=1 => weight shape (48, 24, 3, 3, 3). Input: (8, 48, 96, 96, 96). Output: (8, 24, 98, 98, 98). Output elements: 8 * 24 * 98^3 ≈ 8 * 24 * 941,192 = 8 * 22,580,608 ≈ 180,644,864. Implicit GEMM for conv3d: - Divide the work into tiles - Each thread block computes a tile of output - Load input patches into shared memory - Compute matrix multiplication usingwarp-level operations For 3D convolution with small kernels, direct convolution can also be very efficient: - Each thread computes one output pixel across all output channels (or a small group) - Load the corresponding input neighborhood and weights from global memory - The weights can be cached in registers or shared memory Given that out_channels=24 is relatively small, we can have each thread compute one (batch, d, h, w) location for all 24 output channels. That would require 24 accumulators per thread. - With 128 threads per block, each block computes 128 spatial locations with 24 channels each. Each thread handles 1 spatial location and computes all 24 output channels. - 128 threads * 96 bytes (24 floats) = 12,288 bytes of accumulator registers. Plus input/weight registers. Total maybe 48 registers per thread. For the weight, we can cache it in shared memory or load from global memory. The weight is only 48 * 24 * 27 = 31,104 floats = 124 KB. That's small and fits in L2 cache. We can load it on demand, or preload to shared memory. For the input, each thread needs to load a 3x3x3 neighborhood around its output location (with padding=2). Actually, output at (d_out, h_out, w_out) in the padded input corresponds to input at (d_out, h_out, w_out) but padded. For padding=2, the padded input is 100x100x100. Each output location requires a 3x3x3 region from the padded input. For 128 threads, the union of all required input depends on how the output locations are distributed. If we use a tile of 8x4x4 = 128 output locations, the input footprint is roughly (8+2)x(4+2)x(4+2) = 10x6x6 = 360 spatial locations, across all in_channels. That's 360 * 48 * 4 = 69,120 bytes per tile. With shared memory of 227KB, we could easily cache this. So the plan: 1. Preprocess weight in __init__: flip weight in spatial dims and transpose in/out channels to get standard conv3d weight shape (out_c, in_c, K, K, K) 2. Global memory access pattern for the unoptimized kernel: For output at (n, d, h, w), thread reads input at (n, c_in, d-kd, h-kh, w-kw) for all c_in, kd, kh, kw. If threads in a warp have contiguous w, then w-kw is contiguous (for fixed kw). So for each (c_in, kd, kh, kw), the warp reads contiguous memory in W. This gives full coalescing! The memory access is: For each c_in: For kd, kh, kw: read input[n, c_in, d-kd, h-kh, w-kw] for all threads in warp (coalesced) read weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] for c_out=0..23 for each thread (not coalesced within warp, but all threads read the same) Wait, if all threads read the same weight values (broadcast), that's actually very efficient - the value is loaded once per warp and broadcast. For weight, if thread 0 is computing all 24 channels, it reads weight[c_in, 0, kd, kh, kw], weight[c_in, 1, kd, kh, kw], ... sequentially. These are contiguous in memory because weight is [in_channels, out_channels, K, K, K]. So for fixed c_in, kd, kh, kw, the 24 weights are contiguous. So the kernel's memory pattern is actually quite good even without shared memory: - Input: coalesced reads from global memory, good caching due to L2 - Weight: sequential reads per thread, broadcast across warp, good caching The FMAs are 31,104 per thread. Wait, that can't be right. Wait, I made a mistake. The weight is read once per (c_in, kd, kh, kw), but it's read by EVERY thread. So total weight reads across all threads is huge. Total threads: 8 * 98^3 = 7,529,536 threads (if 1 thread per output pixel). Each thread reads 31,104 weight floats. Total weight reads: 7.5M * 31,104 = 234 billion weight reads! But weight is only 31,104 floats total. Without shared memory, each thread reads the entire weight from global memory. This is terrible! With L2 cache, the weight might be cached. 31,104 floats = 124 KB. On H100, L2 cache is 50MB. So weight stays in L2. But L2 bandwidth is limited. L2 bandwidth is typically 2-4x DRAM bandwidth, maybe ~6-10 TB/s. Total weight traffic: 234B floats * 4 bytes = 936 GB. At 10 TB/s, that's 94 ms. That's way too slow! So we absolutely need shared memory or register caching of the weight, or at least to share weight among threads in a block. If we put the entire weight in shared memory (124 KB), and each block reads it once, total weight traffic = 124 KB * number_of_blocks. With 76,832 blocks: 124 KB * 76,832 = 9.5 GB. At 10 TB/s, that's 0.95 ms. Much better! And input traffic: each block reads its tile from global memory. Interior tile (D=4, H=4, W=34) with 48 channels: 4*4*34*48 = 26,112 floats = 104 KB per block. Total input traffic: 104 KB * 76,832 = 8 GB. At 3.35 TB/s: 2.4 ms. Total memory time: ~3.4 ms. Let me draft the CUDA code: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_d, int pad_h, int pad_w, int out_pad_d, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int dil_d, int dil_h, int dil_w, int groups, bool has_bias ) { // Each block handles a tile of output // Each thread handles one output spatial location for all output channels // Thread mapping: // threadIdx.x -> w within tile (up to W_TILE) // threadIdx.y -> h (0 or 1 for H_TILE=2) // threadIdx.z -> d (0 or 1 for D_TILE=2) // But to limit threads per block, we can use 1D threadIdx and compute manually. // Actually, let's use: // int tid = threadIdx.x; // int w_local = tid % W_TILE; // int h_local = (tid / W_TILE) % H_TILE; // int d_local = tid / (W_TILE * H_TILE); const int D_TILE = 2; const int H_TILE = 1; const int W_TILE = 32; const int BLOCK_SIZE = D_TILE * H_TILE * W_TILE; // 64 // ... shared memory for input and weight } ``` Actually, I realize I should finalize the exact approach. For D=8,H=8,W=8 and K=3, tiles start at d=0,8,16,...,96 (actually 96, but 96+8=104 > 98, so last tile starts at 96 and covers 96..97, i.e., size 2? Actually, we launch a grid where each block handles a tile. For output dimensions that aren't multiples of tile size, some threads in edge blocks might be out of bounds. We can handle this with a guard: if (d_out >= D_out) return; For a tile starting at d=96 with D_TILE=8, the thread computes d_out=96..103, but only 96,97 are valid. The rest return early. This means some threads are idle. The fraction of idle threads is small for large outputs (98/8 has last tile with 2 valid out of 8 = 25% idle). Also, for input loading: edge tiles need boundary checks when reading from global memory. This adds complexity. To simplify, I can: 1. Allocate shared memory for the full tile + halo 2. Have all threads in the block cooperatively load input, with boundary checks 3. Some input locations in shared memory (the out-of-bounds ones) are left as zero 4. The main computation loop has NO boundary checks This is a common pattern in conv kernels. Let me formalize the kernel design: ```cuda template<int D_TILE, int H_TILE, int W_TILE, int BLOCK_SIZE> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, bool has_bias ) { // Constants for K=3: we can specialize const int K = 3; const int PAD = K - 1; // 2 // Block info int n = blockIdx.z; // across batches int w_base = blockIdx.x * W_TILE; int h_base = (blockIdx.y % ((H_out + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE)) * H_TILE; int d_base = (blockIdx.y / ((H_out + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE)) * D_TILE; // Or use 3D grid for spatial dimensions } ``` Grid configuration: For simplicity, I can use a 1D grid over blocks and compute n, d_base, h_base, w_base from blockIdx.x. Or use 4D grid: gridDim.x = ceil(W_out / W_TILE), gridDim.y = ceil(H_out / H_TILE), gridDim.z = ceil(D_out / D_TILE) * N. Actually, CUDA grids are at most 3D. So: gridDim.x = ceil(W_out / W_TILE) gridDim.y = ceil(H_out / H_TILE) * ceil(D_out / D_TILE) gridDim.z = N Then: int w_base = blockIdx.x * W_TILE; int dhw_idx = blockIdx.y; int d_idx = dhw_idx % ceil(D_out / D_TILE); int h_idx = dhw_idx / ceil(D_out / D_TILE); int d_base = d_idx * D_TILE; int h_base = h_idx * H_TILE; This works. Shared memory layout: For input with D_smem = D_TILE + K - 1, H_smem = H_TILE + K - 1, W_smem = W_TILE + K - 1. Total input floats: D_smem * H_smem * W_smem * C_in. Wait, for transpose conv with our formula d_in = d_out - kd, the input needed for output tile [d_base, d_base+D_TILE) is [d_base - (K-1), d_base + D_TILE - 1]. But this is clamped to [0, D_in-1]. So the smem size depends on whether we're at the edge. For interior tiles: D_smem = D_TILE + K - 1. For edge tiles: D_smem is smaller. To keep it simple and avoid dynamic smem sizing, I'll allocate the maximum and only load the valid portion. The smem footprint is fixed at (D_TILE + K - 1) * (H_TILE + K - 1) * (W_TILE + K - 1) * C_in. For D_TILE=8, H_TILE=8, W_TILE=8, K=3: D_smem = 10, H_smem = 10, W_smem = 10. Total = 1000 * 48 = 48,000 floats = 192 KB. If I use this, plus maybe no weight in smem, total is 192 KB. Within 227 KB. Good! For the cooperative load: 512 threads loading 48,000 floats. Each thread loads 48,000 / 512 = 93.75 floats. Each float4 load is 4 floats, so ~24 float4 loads per thread. Easy. Actually, for float4 loads, we'd need to pad W_smem to be a multiple of 4 to avoid bank conflicts, or organize smem as [D][H][W][C_in] with W padded to multiple of 4. Actually, to avoid shared memory bank conflicts, if we store as [D][H][W][C_in] and threads in a warp access the same (d,h,w) but different c_in, then contiguous c_in maps to contiguous banks. With 32 banks and float (4 bytes), bank index = (address / 4) % 32. If threads access c_in=0,1,2,...,31 simultaneously, that's bank 0,1,2,...,31. No conflict! If they access the same c_in, that's broadcast. No conflict. So layout [D][H][W][C_in] works well. For cooperative loading, we can have threads load along the C_in dimension. Let me define the smem layout: ```cuda __shared__ float smem_input[(D_TILE+2) * (H_TILE+2) * (W_TILE+2) * C_IN]; ``` Wait, C_IN can't be a template parameter if it's variable. For the fast path, I'll hardcode or make it a constexpr for the specific test case. Since in_channels=48 is known from the test, I can use it as a template parameter. Actually, to make the kernel flexible while optimized, I can use template parameters for the specific case and a generic loop for other cases. But function templates with varying C_in aren't easily instantiated via pybind11. Alternative: pass C_in as a runtime parameter, but let the compiler optimize by using the fact that loops over C_in have trip count known at grid launch? No, the compiler won't unroll loops with runtime bounds. For the test case, I can write a fully specialized kernel where C_in=48, C_out=24, K=3, D_in=96, etc. are known at compile time. This would allow maximum optimization. But if the benchmark varies these parameters, the kernel would crash. To be safe, I'll write a kernel that accepts all parameters at runtime, but within it, I can use `if constexpr` style optimization or just write the general loop and hope the compiler does well. For H100 and these sizes, the general loop might still be competitive. Actually, since the test only uses specific values and the benchmark might test with those exact values or small variations, I can write a kernel with template specializations for common sizes and instantiate them. But `load_inline` with pybind doesn't easily support template instantiation. I'd need to write wrapper functions for each template instance. Let me do this: write a generic kernel that accepts runtime parameters. Use `int` for sizes and trust the compiler with loop unrolling pragmas. For the important inner loops (kd, kh, kw), I'll always use `#pragma unroll` since K is typically small. For C_in and C_out loops, I'll keep them as loops but try to help the compiler. Actually, wait. I can use a neat trick: declare the kernel as a template with default non-type parameters, but instantiate just one version with the test parameters. No, that won't handle other test cases. OK, I'll write a flexible kernel but with `#pragma unroll` for K. For C_in and C_out, I'll make the inner loop compute multiple channels using vectorized load/store. Here's the plan for the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_fast( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int groups, bool has_bias ) { const int D_TILE = 8; const int H_TILE = 8; const int W_TILE = 8; const int BLOCK_SIZE = D_TILE * H_TILE * W_TILE; // 512? Too many threads maybe. // Actually 512 threads per block is fine. } ``` Wait, let me check H100 occupancy with 512 threads per block and 192 KB shared memory: - Max threads per SM: 2048. So 4 blocks per SM max (512*4=2048). - Max shared memory per SM: 228 KB. With 192 KB per block, only 1 block per SM. - So occupancy is limited by shared memory: 1 block per SM = 512 threads / 2048 max = 25% occupancy. With 132 SMs, total concurrent blocks = 132. Total blocks = ceil(98/8)*ceil(98/8)*ceil(98/8)*8 = 13*13*13*8 = 17,576. Waves = 17,576 / 132 = 133 waves. At 25% occupancy, we rely on instruction-level parallelism within warps. For FMA-heavy code, this is usually fine. But can we improve occupancy by using less shared memory? What if we process fewer channels per block, or use a different strategy? If we use only input in shared memory (192 KB) and load weights from L2, we can't reduce input much for D_TILE=8,H_TILE=8,W_TILE=8. What about W_TILE=16, H_TILE=4, D_TILE=4? Smem = 6*6*18*48*4 = 124,416 bytes = 121.5 KB. Total = 121.5 KB. With 121.5 KB shared memory, we can fit 1 block per SM (still). But block_size = 4*4*16 = 256 threads. Blocks: ceil(98/4)*ceil(98/4)*ceil(98/16)*8 = 25*25*7*8 = 35,000. Occupancy: 256 threads per block. Shared memory per block 121.5 KB. Still 1 block per SM = 256 threads = 12.5% occupancy. Worse. What about D_TILE=4, H_TILE=4, W_TILE=16? Smem = 6*6*18*48*4 = 124,416 bytes. Block = 256 threads. Same as above. To get 2 blocks per SM, shared memory must be < 114 KB. Smem = 114 KB means (D+2)(H+2)(W+2)*48*4 <= 114*1024. (D+2)(H+2)(W+2) <= 608. D=4,H=4,W=8: 6*6*10 = 360. Smem = 67.5 KB. Total = 67.5 KB. With block = 128 threads, we can fit 3 blocks per SM (128*3 = 384 threads, 67.5*3 = 202.5 KB). Or 2 blocks = 256 threads, 135 KB. Blocks: ceil(98/4)^2 * ceil(98/8) * 8 = 25*25*13*8 = 65,000. Occupancy with 2 blocks per SM: 256 threads / 2048 = 12.5%. Hmm. Actually, to get better occupancy, we want smaller blocks (like 128 threads) with less shared memory so 2-4 can fit. With 67.5 KB per block, we can fit 3 blocks per SM: 384 threads / 2048 = 18.75%. With D=8,H=8,W=8 and 192 KB, only 1 block per SM. But 512 threads = 25% occupancy per SM, which is actually better than 384/2048 = 18.75%. So D=8,H=8,W=8 with 512 threads might be better despite the larger shared memory. What if we use D=4,H=4,W=8 (128 threads, 67.5 KB) and have 3 blocks per SM? Per SM: 384 threads, total concurrent blocks = 132 * 3 = 396. Waves: 65,000 / 396 = 164 waves. Per block FMAs: 128 * C_out * C_in * 27. For C_out=24, C_in=48: 128*24*48*27 = 3.98M FMAs. Time per block at 67 TFLOPS: 3.98M / 67G = 59 microseconds. Total time (ignoring parallelization): 65,000 * 59 us. No, that's wrong. Total FMAs = 468 GFLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS: 7 ms. With 132 SMs at 25% occupancy: effective throughput might be ~16 TFLOPS. Time = 468 / 16 = 29 ms. That's awful. Wait, I think I'm confusing myself with occupancy calculations. H100 at 25% occupancy of 512 threads can still sustain near-peak FLOPS if the warps are compute-bound with enough ILP. The occupancy percentage doesn't directly limit FLOPS - it limits latency hiding. For a compute-bound kernel with independent instructions, even 25% occupancy can get near peak. For matrix multiplication, typical occupancy is 25-50% and it achieves near-peak performance. So I shouldn't worry too much about occupancy as long as we have enough warps per SM to hide memory latency. For shared-memory-heavy kernels, the latency is low anyway. Let me just go with D_TILE=8, H_TILE=8, W_TILE=8, BLOCK_SIZE=512, and shared memory for input only (192 KB). This gives us: - Good amount of work per block - Reasonable shared memory usage - Coalesced memory access Now let me think about the actual CUDA code. For the cooperative load of input: ```cuda // Shared memory for input tile + halo extern __shared__ float smem[]; // We need to load from global memory to smem // Global input coordinate for smem[d][h][w][c]: // d_g = d_base + d - 2 (for K=3, so d ranges 0..D_TILE+1) // But clamped to [0, D_in-1] // Actually for TransposeConv: // Output at d_out needs input at d_in = d_out - kd // The input smem stores values at various d_in, h_in, w_in // For a given output tile at d_base..d_base+D_TILE-1, and kd=0..2: // d_in ranges from d_base - 2 to d_base + D_TILE - 1 ``` Wait, if we want to pre-load input into smem and then access without boundary checks in the compute loop, the smem needs to cover the range [d_base-2, d_base+D_TILE-1] for D, and similarly for H and W. The size of this range is D_TILE + 2. For D_TILE=8, size=10. But for edge tiles where d_base=0, the range is [-2, 7], which is clamped to [0, 7]. So d_in ranges 0..7, size=8. But our smem still allocates 10 entries. We can pad the missing entries with 0. So the loading code is: ```cuda for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < D_SMEM * H_SMEM * W_SMEM * C_in; idx += blockDim.x) { // decode idx to c, d_s, h_s, w_s int c = idx % C_in; int tmp = idx / C_in; int w_s = tmp % W_SMEM; tmp /= W_SMEM; int h_s = tmp % H_SMEM; int d_s = tmp / H_SMEM; int d_in = d_base + d_s - (K - 1); int h_in = h_base + h_s - (K - 1); int w_in = w_base + w_s - (K - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (d_in >= 0 && d_in < D_in && h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + c) * D_in + d_in) * H_in * W_in + h_in * W_in + w_in]; } // Store in smem // Layout: [d_s][h_s][w_s][c] or [c][d_s][h_s][w_s] // Let's use [d_s][h_s][w_s][c] to allow vectorized access for channel loops smem[((d_s * H_SMEM + h_s) * W_SMEM + w_s) * C_in + c] = val; } __syncthreads(); ``` Then the compute loop: ```cuda // Each thread computes one (or more) output locations int tid = threadIdx.x; int w_local = tid % W_TILE; int h_local = (tid / W_TILE) % H_TILE; int d_local = tid / (W_TILE * H_TILE); int d_out = d_base + d_local; int h_out = h_base + h_local; int w_out = w_base + w_local; if (d_out >= D_out || h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; float acc[24]; // For C_out=24 if (has_bias) { for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) acc[co] = bias[co]; } else { for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) acc[co] = 0.0f; } for (int c = 0; c < C_in; c++) { #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; kd++) { int d_s = d_local + kd; // input smem index (since d_in = d_out - kd, and d_out = d_base + d_local, d_in in smem is at d_s = d_local + kd) #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int h_s = h_local + kh; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int w_s = w_local + kw; float inp = smem[((d_s * H_SMEM + h_s) * W_SMEM + w_s) * C_in + c]; #pragma unroll for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { acc[co] += inp * weight[((c * C_out + co) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw]; } } } } } ``` Wait, the weight layout in ConvTranspose3d is [in_channels, out_channels, K, K, K]. So weight_idx = ((c * C_out + co) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw. For the inner co loop, c and kd,kh,kw are fixed. The access pattern is weight[c, co, kd, kh, kw] for co=0..23. Since C_out=24, these are contiguous! Great for memory access. But all threads in a warp access different spatial locations, so they access different `inp` values from shared memory. The shared memory accesses for `inp` depend on w_local. If w_local varies across threads in a warp, and the smem layout has C_in as the innermost dimension, then for a given (d_s, h_s, w_s), threads access different w_s values. With w_local contiguous across threads, w_s = w_local + kw is also contiguous. And since C_in is the innermost dimension, for a fixed c, the addresses are separated by C_in. Wait, that's strided access, not contiguous. Smem layout: [d_s][h_s][w_s][c] Address of [d_s][h_s][w_s][c] = ((d_s * H + h_s) * W + w_s) * C_in + c For fixed d_s, h_s, c, and varying w_s: addresses are separated by C_in = 48 floats = 192 bytes. Bank width is 4 bytes, 32 banks. So consecutive w_s values map to banks: bank = (address / 4) % 32 = (((base + w_s * C_in) * 4) / 4) % 32 = (base + w_s * 48) % 32 = (base + w_s * 16) % 32. (since 48 % 32 = 16) For w_s = 0, 1, 2, ..., 31: banks = base, base+16, base+0, base+16, ... (for even/odd w_s) Wait, w_s=0: base w_s=1: base+16 w_s=2: base+32 ≡ base w_s=3: base+48 ≡ base+16 ... So even w_s map to one bank group, odd w_s map to another. With 32 threads in a warp, half access bank X and half access bank X+16. This causes 2-way bank conflicts! Not ideal. To fix this, I can pad W_SMEM so that W_SMEM * C_in is a multiple of 32. W_SMEM = 10, C_in = 48. W_SMEM * C_in = 480. 480 % 32 = 0! Wait, 480 / 32 = 15. So bank = (base + w_s * 480) % 32 = base % 32 for all w_s? No, because address = base + w_s * 480. Since 480 ≡ 0 mod 32, all w_s map to the same bank! That's a 32-way bank conflict! DISASTER! We need to change the smem layout. Options: 1. Make C_in the outermost dimension and loop over c inside the w loop. Layout: [c][d_s][h_s][w_s] Address = (c * D_SMEM * H_SMEM * W_SMEM + d_s * H_SMEM * W_SMEM + h_s * W_SMEM + w_s) For fixed c, d_s, h_s, varying w_s: addresses are base, base+1, base+2,... contiguous. Bank = base % 32. For w_s = 0..31 in a warp: banks = base%32, (base+1)%32, ..., (base+31)%32. All different = no bank conflicts! PERFECT! 2. Add padding to the innermost dimension so that stride between w_s values is a multiple of 32 but not dividing the address space evenly. Actually, we want (W_SMEM_padded * C_in) % 32 != 0, and ideally relatively prime to 32. If C_in=48, and we use W_SMEM_padded=11, then stride = 528. 528 % 32 = 16. This causes 2-way conflicts as I showed earlier. If W_SMEM_padded=13, stride=624. 624 % 32 = 16. Same. What if we change the layout to [d_s][h_s][c][w_s]? For W_SMEM=10, stride = 1. No conflicts! But then for fixed d_s, h_s, w_s, varying c: addresses are base, base+W_SMEM, base+2*W_SMEM... 10 % 32 = 10. So c and c+32 would conflict? With C_in=48 < 32? Actually C_in=48 > 32. c=0 accesses bank 0, c=1 accesses bank 10, c=2 accesses bank 20, c=3 accesses bank 30, c=4 accesses bank 8, c=5 accesses bank 18, c=6 accesses bank 28, c=7 accesses bank 6... For 48 channels, multiple c values map to same bank. If a warp accesses different c values, there are bank conflicts. Let me think about the access patterns: In the compute loop: ```cuda for (int c = 0; c < C_in; c++) { for (kd,kh,kw) { float inp = smem[...][c][...]; // all threads access same c for (co) { acc[co] += inp * weight... } } } ``` All threads in a warp access the same c value at the same time. They access the same c but different (d_s, h_s, w_s). So the varying dimension is w_s. Option A: Layout [c][d_s][h_s][w_s] For varying w_s, fixed c, d_s, h_s: addresses are contiguous. Banks = base, base+1, base+2,... No conflicts for w_s in [0,31]. This is ideal for our warp access pattern! But the smem size is C_in * D_SMEM * H_SMEM * W_SMEM. With C_in=48, D=10, H=10, W=10: 48,000 floats. Wait, for the loading phase: all threads in a block cooperate to load. If thread t loads address t, t+BLOCK_SIZE, etc., we'd want coalesced global memory access. With layout [c][d_s][h_s][w_s], global memory is also [n][c][d][h][w]. So loading w_s contiguously gives coalesced global access. And with layout [c][d_s][h_s][w_s], adjacent threads load adjacent w_s for the same c, d_s, h_s. This is coalesced! So the ideal layout is [c][d_s][h_s][w_s]. Let me verify with specific numbers: - smem size for each (c, d_s, h_s) slice: W_SMEM floats. - Address of element: (c * D_SMEM * H_SMEM + d_s * H_SMEM + h_s) * W_SMEM + w_s. For the compute phase, with threads accessing different w_s but same c, d_s, h_s: thread i accesses w_s = w_local_i + kw. If w_local_i are contiguous across threads, then w_s are contiguous. Banks are (base + w_s) % 32. For w_s in [0,31], all 32 banks are accessed. No conflict! For the load phase, thread t loads: int idx = t; while (idx < total) { int w_s = idx % W_SMEM; int tmp = idx / W_SMEM; int h_s = tmp % H_SMEM; int d_s = (tmp / H_SMEM) % D_SMEM; int c = tmp / (H_SMEM * D_SMEM); // load from global at (n, c, d_in, h_in, w_in) // store to smem at linear address idx idx += blockDim.x; } Since adjacent threads load adjacent w_s (for same c,d_s,h_s), global reads are coalesced. And smem writes are also to adjacent addresses, so no bank conflicts. Great! This layout is perfect. Now for the compute loop, since all threads in a warp access the same c at each step, and we have 48 channels, should we restructure to have threads cooperatively load input for the current c? Actually, input is already in smem. Each thread just reads from smem. What about register pressure? Each thread has 24 accumulators. That's 24 registers. Plus loop variables, plus shared memory address calculations. Maybe 32-40 registers. Well within 255 limit. But 512 threads per block * 40 registers = 20,480 registers. Per SM max is 64K. So 3 blocks per SM. But shared memory limits us to 1 block per SM. Register count is not the bottleneck. One more optimization: the `co` loop over 24 channels. Can we vectorize this? For the weight read, weights for co=0..23 are contiguous. We can load them as float4 (6 float4 loads) to improve memory throughput. However, the compiler might already vectorize this. If we manually unroll with float4: ```cuda float4 w4 = * reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(&weight[base + co]); acc[co+0] += inp * w4.x; acc[co+1] += inp * w4.y; acc[co+2] += inp * w4.z; acc[co+3] += inp * w4.w; ``` But we need to ensure 16-byte alignment for float4 loads. The weight tensor from PyTorch should be 16-byte aligned for the first element of each contiguous run. For the innermost dimension being C_out=24, and base address for (c, kd, kh, kw) starting at weight[((c * C_out + 0) * ...], this address is aligned if the tensor's c_out stride makes it so. The weight is [C_in, C_out, K, K, K]. For fixed c and kd,kh,kw, the weights for co=0..23 are contiguous. The base address might not be 16-byte aligned unless we specifically align the tensor, or unless C_out * K^3 makes it align. Actually, the weight tensor from nn.ConvTranspose3d is contiguous. The address of weight[c, 0, kd, kh, kw] is: offset = ((c * C_out + 0) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw For this to be 16-byte aligned, offset must be divisible by 4. offset = (c * C_out * K^3 + kd * K^2 + kh * K + kw) For K=3: offset = c * C_out * 27 + kd * 9 + kh * 3 + kw For c=0, kd=0, kh=0, kw=0: offset=0. Aligned. For c=0, kd=0, kh=0, kw=1: offset=1. Not divisible by 4! So only the first element is guaranteed aligned. Subsequent elements within the same (c, kd, kh, kw) group for varying co are contiguous but not 16-byte aligned. Wait, offset is in floats. For float4 alignment, the byte offset must be divisible by 16, which means the float offset must be divisible by 4. offset = c * C_out * 27 + kd * 9 + kh * 3 + kw For kw=0: offset = c * 648 + kd * 9 + kh * 3. Is this always divisible by 4? c * 648: 648 % 4 = 0, yes. kd * 9: 9 % 4 = 1, so depends on kd. kh * 3: 3 % 4 = 3. kd=0, kh=0: offset=0, divisible by 4. Good. kd=0, kh=1: offset=3, not divisible by 4. kd=1, kh=0: offset=9, not divisible by 4. So alignment varies. We can't safely use float4 for all positions without alignment checks. The compiler might generate vectorized loads when it can prove alignment. Or we can manually handle alignment with a scalar prefix, then float4 loop, then scalar suffix. But this adds complexity. For now, let me not use float4 and trust the compiler. Actually, another optimization: since we're memory-bound on weight reads from global memory, using float4 would reduce the number of memory instructions and improve throughput. And since H100 has L2 cache, even unaligned float4 loads can be fast (though slightly less efficient). But to be safe and correct, I'll avoid manual float4 unless needed. Let me now consider: what if I partition C_in among threads? Each thread computes a subset of C_in for its output pixel, then we reduce? This would increase parallelism but also increase inter-thread communication. For example, 512 threads in a block. Each thread could compute 1 output pixel for all C_out=24 channels, but only for C_in/8 = 6 input channels. Then we need to reduce across 8 threads. With warp shuffles or shared memory, this is possible but adds overhead. Actually, since C_in=48, we could have 8 groups of 64 threads. Each group of 64 threads handles 64 spatial locations * 6 input channels. Then reduce across the 8 groups for each spatial location. But the reduction requires synchronization and extra shared memory. For 64 locations * 24 channels, the partial sums per group are 64*24 = 1,536 floats. With 8 groups, total partial sums = 12,288 floats = 48 KB. This fits in shared memory. But the complexity is high. Let me avoid this for now. Let me write the Python + CUDA code now. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # CUDA source cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, bool has_bias ) { // Tile sizes const int D_TILE = 8; int h_idx = spatial_idx % num_h_tiles; int d_idx = spatial_idx / num_h_tiles; int h_base = h_idx * H_TILE; int d_base = d_idx * D_TILE; // Shared memory for input tile + halo const int D_SMEM = D_TILE + 2; int d_in = d_base + d_s - (K - 1); int h_in = h_base + h_s - (K - 1); int w_in = w_base + w_s - (K - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (d_in >= 0 && d_in < D_in && h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + c) * D_in + d_in) * H_in * W_in + h_in * W_in + w_in]; The kernel has this inner loop: ```cuda for c in 0..47: for kd in 0..2: for kh in 0..2: for kw in 0..2: inp = smem[...] for co in 0..23: acc[co] = fma(inp, weight[...], acc[co]) ``` 27 iterations of (load + 24 FMAs) per channel. Total 48 * 27 = 1,296 iterations. Each iteration does 1 smem load and 24 FMAs. The FMAs have a latency of ~4 cycles on H100, and throughput of 2 per cycle per thread (for FP32). So each FMA takes 0.5 cycles. 24 FMAs = 12 cycles. The smem load has ~20-30 cycles latency but is initiated once per 24 FMAs. With sufficient ILP (24 independent FMA chains), the compute should overlap the memory latency. Since we have 24 independent accumulators, the compiler should be able to schedule instructions to hide most of the smem latency. The weight loads from global memory (which we're doing) have higher latency (~100-400 cycles), but with L2 cache and 24 FMAs to hide behind, it should be okay. Actually, in the inner loop, the weight is loaded from global memory: ```cuda int w_offset = ((c * C_out + 0) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw; #pragma unroll for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { acc[co] += inp * weight[w_offset + co]; } ``` The compiler will load `weight[w_offset + co]` for each co. Since these are 24 contiguous floats from L2 cache, the loads can be pipelined. However, the compiler might not be smart enough to vectorize these loads or to schedule them optimally. For the general kernel, here's a simple implementation: - Each thread computes one output pixel for all output channels - Direct formula with all boundary checks ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_general_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_d, int pad_h, int pad_w, int out_pad_d, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int dil_d, int dil_h, int dil_w, int groups, bool has_bias ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; // Decode idx to n, c_out, d_out, h_out, w_out int w_out = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h_out = tmp % H_out; tmp /= H_out; int d_out = tmp % D_out; int c_out = tmp / D_out; int n = c_out / C_out; c_out = c_out % C_out; // Adjust for output padding? For each output position, compute the ConvTranspose3d formula directly 3. Use a simple loop Let me look up the exact formula for ConvTranspose3d to make sure I get it right. For ConvTranspose3d, the relationship between input and output positions for stride=1: out[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k} in[n, c_in, d-k_d + pad_d, h-k_h + pad_h, w-k_w + pad_w] * weight[c_in, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] For general stride: The effective formula is: first expand input by stride (insert zeros), then convolve. For output (d, h, w), it receives contributions from input (d', h', w') where: d = d' * stride_d - pad_d + k_d * dil_d h = h' * stride_h - pad_h + k_h * dil_h w = w' * stride_w - pad_w + k_w * dil_w So d' = (d + pad_d - k_d * dil_d) / stride_d This must be an integer and within bounds. The algorithm is: for each (n, c_out, d, h, w): acc = bias[c_out] for c_in_group: for kd, kh, kw: d_in_numer = d + pad_d - kd * dil_d; h_in_numer = h + pad_h - kh * dil_h; w_in_numer = w + pad_w - kw * dil_w; if (d_in_numer % stride_d != 0) continue; // no contribution d_in = d_in_numer / stride_d; // similarly for h, w if (d_in < 0 || d_in >= D_in) continue; float val = input[n, c_in, d_in, h_in, w_in]; acc += val * weight[c_in_group, c_out_group, kd, kh, kw]; output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = acc; For groups, C_in per group is C_in / groups, and C_out per group is C_out / groups. This general formula is straightforward. I'll write a general kernel using this. But there's a catch: for `output_padding`, the output size is increased. The formula for size is: D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride_d - 2*pad_d + dil_d*(K-1) + output_padding_d + 1 But the actual computation doesn't use `output_padding` in the kernel loop directly; the output is just larger. Wait, that seems wrong. Let me verify with PyTorch docs. From PyTorch docs for ConvTranspose3d: "The padding argument effectively adds dilation * (kernel_size - 1) - padding amount of zero padding to both sizes of the input. This is set so that when a Conv3d and a ConvTranspose3d are initialized with same parameters, they are inverses in regard to the input and output shapes." And: "output_padding controls the amount of implicit zero-paddings on both sides of the output for output_padding number of points for each dimension." "output_padding is only used to find output shape, but does not actually add zero-padding to output." So output_padding just affects the output size, it doesn't change the kernel computation. The extra output elements are still computed by the transpose convolution formula. Actually wait, that means for some output positions that are in the "output_padding" region, the formula just happens to produce values, but some positions at the very end might have no valid input contributions and would be zero? Or does it mean the formula naturally covers the extended size? I think the safest approach is: compute output size with the formula, then for each output position use the general transpose conv formula. Don't special-case output_padding in the kernel. OK, now let me write the actual code. For the general kernel, I'll use a thread-per-output-element approach with no shared memory. For the fast kernel, I'll use the shared-memory approach with the K=3, stride=1, pad=0 specialization. Let me draft the full Python module now. One more issue: for the fast kernel with shared memory, we need to `extern __shared__ float smem[];` and pass the size at kernel launch. But for kernels with template parameters or compile-time sizes, `extern __shared__` is fine. Actually, in CUDA, you can't have `extern __shared__` together with template parameters easily... wait, yes you can. The shared memory size is determined by the third argument to `<<<grid, block, smem>>>`. Let me write the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = ''' // Not needed ''' cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> // Fast path kernel for K=3, stride=1, pad=0, dilation=1, groups=1, output_padding=0 __global__ void conv_transpose3d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out ) { const int K = 3; const int D_TILE = 4; const int H_TILE = 4; const int W_TILE = 16; const int BLOCK_SIZE = D_TILE * H_TILE * W_TILE; // 256 int n = blockIdx.z; int w_base = blockIdx.x * W_TILE; int spatial_idx = blockIdx.y; int num_h_tiles = (H_out + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE; int num_d_tiles = (D_out + D_TILE - 1) / D_TILE; int h_idx = spatial_idx % num_h_tiles; int d_idx = spatial_idx / num_h_tiles; int h_base = h_idx * H_TILE; int d_base = d_idx * D_TILE; const int D_SMEM = D_TILE + K - 1; // 6 const int H_SMEM = H_TILE + K - 1; // 6 const int W_SMEM = W_TILE + K - 1; // 18 extern __shared__ float smem[]; const int SMEM_VOLUME = D_SMEM * H_SMEM * W_SMEM; // Cooperative load of input int total_load = C_in * SMEM_VOLUME; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_load; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { int w_s = idx % W_SMEM; int tmp = idx / W_SMEM; int h_s = tmp % H_SMEM; int d_s = (tmp / H_SMEM) % D_SMEM; int c = tmp / (H_SMEM * D_SMEM); int d_in = d_base + d_s - (K - 1); int h_in = h_base + h_s - (K - 1); int w_in = w_base + w_s - (K - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (d_in >= 0 && d_in < D_in && h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + c) * D_in + d_in) * H_in * W_in + h_in * W_in + w_in]; } smem[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); int tid = threadIdx.x; int w_local = tid % W_TILE; int h_local = (tid / W_TILE) % H_TILE; int d_local = tid / (W_TILE * H_TILE); int w_out = w_base + w_local; int h_out = h_base + h_local; int d_out = d_base + d_local; if (d_out >= D_out || h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; float acc[24]; if (C_out <= 24) { for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) acc[co] = bias ? bias[co] : 0.0f; } for (int c = 0; c < C_in; c++) { #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < K; kd++) { int d_s = d_local + kd; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { int h_s = h_local + kh; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int w_s = w_local + kw; float inp = smem[((c * D_SMEM + d_s) * H_SMEM + h_s) * W_SMEM + w_s]; int w_base_idx = ((c * C_out) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw; #pragma unroll for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { acc[co] += inp * weight[w_base_idx + co]; } } } } } int out_idx = ((n * C_out) * D_out + d_out) * H_out * W_out + h_out * W_out + w_out; int out_stride = D_out * H_out * W_out; for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { output[out_idx + co * out_stride] = acc[co]; } } // General kernel __global__ void conv_transpose3d_general_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_d, int pad_h, int pad_w, int out_pad_d, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int dil_d, int dil_h, int dil_w, int groups, bool has_bias ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w_out = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h_out = tmp % H_out; tmp /= H_out; int d_out = tmp % D_out; int c_out = tmp / D_out; int n = c_out / C_out; c_out = c_out % C_out; int g = c_out / (C_out / groups); int c_out_g = c_out % (C_out / groups); int C_in_g = C_in / groups; float acc = has_bias ? bias[c_out] : 0.0f; for (int c_in_g = 0; c_in_g < C_in_g; c_in_g++) { int c_in = g * C_in_g + c_in_g; for (int kd = 0; kd < K; kd++) { int d_in_numer = d_out + pad_d - kd * dil_d; if (d_in_numer < 0 || d_in_numer % stride_d != 0) continue; int d_in = d_in_numer / stride_d; if (d_in >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { int h_in_numer = h_out + pad_h - kh * dil_h; if (h_in_numer < 0 || h_in_numer % stride_h != 0) continue; int h_in = h_in_numer / stride_h; if (h_in >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int w_in_numer = w_out + pad_w - kw * dil_w; if (w_in_numer < 0 || w_in_numer % stride_w != 0) continue; int w_in = w_in_numer / stride_w; if (w_in >= W_in) continue; float inp = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * D_in + d_in) * H_in * W_in + h_in * W_in + w_in]; float wgt = weight[((c_in * (C_out/groups) + c_out_g) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw]; acc += inp * wgt; } } } } output[((n * C_out + c_out) * D_out + d_out) * H_out * W_out + h_out * W_out + w_out] = acc; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_d, int pad_h, int pad_w, int out_pad_d, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int dil_d, int dil_h, int dil_w, int groups ) { auto N = input.size(0); auto C_in = input.size(1); auto D_in = input.size(2); auto H_in = input.size(3); auto W_in = input.size(4); auto C_out_per_group = weight.size(1); auto K = weight.size(2); auto C_out = C_out_per_group * groups; auto D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride_d - 2 * pad_d + dil_d * (K - 1) + out_pad_d + 1; auto H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride_h - 2 * pad_h + dil_h * (K - 1) + out_pad_h + 1; auto W_out = (W_in - 1) * stride_w - 2 * pad_w + dil_w * (K - 1) + out_pad_w + 1; auto output = torch::empty({N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); bool has_bias = bias.has_value(); if (stride_d == 1 && stride_h == 1 && stride_w == 1 && pad_d == 0 && pad_h == 0 && pad_w == 0 && out_pad_d == 0 && out_pad_h == 0 && out_pad_w == 0 && dil_d == 1 && dil_h == 1 && dil_w == 1 && groups == 1) { const int D_TILE = 4; const int H_TILE = 4; const int W_TILE = 16; const int BLOCK_SIZE = D_TILE * H_TILE * W_TILE; dim3 grid( (W_out + W_TILE - 1) / W_TILE, ((H_out + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE) * ((D_out + D_TILE - 1) / D_TILE), N ); int D_SMEM = D_TILE + K - 1; int H_SMEM = H_TILE + K - 1; int W_SMEM = W_TILE + K - 1; size_t smem_size = C_in * D_SMEM * H_SMEM * W_SMEM * sizeof(float); // Set max dynamic shared memory if needed if (smem_size > 48 * 1024) { cudaFuncSetAttribute(conv_transpose3d_fast_kernel, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, smem_size); } conv_transpose3d_fast_kernel<<<grid, BLOCK_SIZE, smem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), has_bias ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out ); } else { int total = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; int threads = 256; int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; conv_transpose3d_general_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), has_bias ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out, K, stride_d, stride_h, stride_w, pad_d, pad_h, pad_w, out_pad_d, out_pad_h, out_pad_w, dil_d, dil_h, dil_w, groups, has_bias ); } return output; } ''' module = load_inline( name='conv_transpose3d_cuda', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['conv_transpose3d_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=True, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, dilation=1, groups=1, bias=False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.stride = stride if isinstance(stride, tuple) else (stride, stride, stride) self.padding = padding if isinstance(padding, tuple) else (padding, padding, padding) self.output_padding = output_padding if isinstance(output_padding, tuple) else (output_padding, output_padding, output_padding) self.dilation = dilation if isinstance(dilation, tuple) else (dilation, dilation, dilation) self.groups = groups self.has_bias = bias self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(in_channels, out_channels // groups, kernel_size, kernel_size, kernel_size)) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) # Initialize like PyTorch nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=sqrt(5)) if self.bias is not None: fan_in = in_channels * kernel_size * kernel_size * kernel_size bound = 1 / sqrt(fan_in) nn.init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) def forward(self, x): return module.conv_transpose3d_cuda( x, self.weight, self.bias if self.has_bias else None, self.stride[0], self.stride[1], self.stride[2], self.padding[0], self.padding[1], self.padding[2], self.output_padding[0], self.output_padding[1], self.output_padding[2], self.dilation[0], self.dilation[1], self.dilation[2], self.groups ) ``` Wait, I need to import math for sqrt. Also, I need to make sure the weight initialization matches PyTorch's. Actually, PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d uses a specific initialization. PyTorch uses a cross-correlation style. For ConvTranspose3d, the effective operation is the gradient of conv3d with respect to input. Let me verify the weight indexing is correct. PyTorch ConvTranspose3d formula (simplified): output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_c_in sum_k x[n, c_in, d', h', w'] * w[c_in, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] where d' and k are related by the stride/padding/dilation formula. For stride=1, pad=0, dil=1: d = d' + k_d, so d' = d - k_d. So: output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_c_in sum_kd,kh,kw x[n, c_in, d-kd, h-kh, w-kw] * w[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] This matches my fast kernel: ```cuda d_in = d_out - kd; ... acc += input[...d_in...] * weight[c, co, kd, kh, kw]; ``` Yes! Bank = (base + w_s) % 32. All distinct. For kw=1, w_s=1..16. Still mostly distinct, but w_s=16 maps to bank (base+16)%32, while w_s=0 would have mapped to base%32. For the 1-channel-per-thread approach, each thread has only 1 accumulator. The loop is: ```cuda for c in 0..47: for kd,kh,kw: inp = smem[...] acc += inp * weight[c, this_co, kd, kh, kw] ``` Only 1 FMA chain. Latency of FMA is ~4 cycles. To hide this, we need 4 independent FMAs in flight. With only 1 accumulator, the next FMA depends on the current one. So the compiler can't hide latency well. For the 24-channel-per-thread approach, there are 24 independent FMA chains. The compiler can interleave them to hide latency. This is much better for ILP. So despite lower occupancy, the 24-channel-per-thread kernel might have better ILP and thus higher throughput. This is a classic GPU optimization tradeoff. For matrix multiply, the optimal block size typically balances occupancy and ILP. Let me try both approaches and see which is faster through experimentation. These are fine with the PyTorch headers that `load_inline` includes. Let me write the full code now. One optimization I want to add: for the fast kernel K=3, I can explicitly unroll with `#pragma unroll` since K=3 is known. And since C_out=24 and C_in=48 are runtime values, I can add template parameters for these too, or I can add special paths. Actually, let me think: if I write `acc[24]` in the kernel, but C_out might be different at runtime, this is invalid. I should use a dynamically sized accumulator. But for performance with C_out=24, I want the compiler to unroll the co loop. I can handle this by checking if C_out <= 32 and using a local array of size 32, then only using the first C_out elements. The compiler should optimize away unused elements. ```cuda float acc[32]; for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) acc[co] = bias ? bias[co] : 0.0f; ``` If C_out is known to be 24 through profiling/static analysis? No, the compiler doesn't know. But `C_out` is a kernel argument (runtime). The loop `for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++)` with `C_out = 24` in practice will execute 24 times. The compiler might partially unroll it if there's a `#pragma unroll`. Actually, modern NVCC with `-O3` might be able to optimize if `C_out` is passed as a kernel parameter and always 24 for a given kernel instance? No, the compiler generates one binary for all possible C_out values. So it can't fully unroll based on the parameter. To enforce unrolling, I'd need to make C_out a template parameter. But I'm not sure how much unrolling matters here. The overhead of a loop with 24 iterations is minimal if the body is primarily FMAs. The FMAs themselves dominate the instruction count. Each iteration of `co` loop does: load weight, FMA, store to register. For 24 iterations, that's 24 loads and 24 FMAs. A loop adds a branch per iteration, but it can be pipelined. The overhead is maybe 1-2 instructions per iteration, which is ~10% of the FMAs. Not huge. For K loops with 3 iterations, unrolling is more important because the loop overhead is a larger fraction. OK, I'll proceed without templating C_in, C_out, and just hardcode K=3 unrolling. Actually, one more idea: I can reorder the loops to improve performance. Currently: ```cuda for c in 0..47: for kd in 0..2: for kh in 0..2: for kw in 0..2: load inp from smem for co in 0..C_out-1: acc[co] += inp * weight[co] ``` What if I reorder to: ```cuda for kd in 0..2: for kh in 0..2: for kw in 0..2: for c in 0..47: load inp from smem for co in 0..C_out-1: acc[co] += inp * weight[co] ``` This changes the access pattern for weight. Previously, for fixed c, the weight access was `weight[c, 0..23, kd, kh, kw]` which is contiguous (24 floats). With the reordered loop, for fixed kd,kh,kw, and varying c, the weight access is `weight[c, 0..23, kd, kh, kw]`. For c=0: base_0 = 0 * C_out * 27 + kd*9 + kh*3 + kw. For c=1: base_1 = 1 * C_out * 27 + kd*9 + kh*3 + kw = base_0 + C_out * 27 = base_0 + 648. This is strided by 648 floats = 2,592 bytes. In terms of cache lines (128 bytes), this is 20 cache lines apart. Not contiguous! Bad for cache locality. So the original order (c outer, co inner) is better for weight access because co is contiguous for fixed c. However, from the smem perspective, the original order accesses smem[c][d_s][h_s][w_s] for varying c. With layout [c][d][h][w], varying c means strided access by D*H*W floats. For D=4,H=4,W=34: D*H*W = 544 floats = 2,176 bytes. Also strided. Hmm, both orders have strided access for one of the tensors. Which is worse? - Weight strided by 648 floats: might require loading a new cache line per c. - Smem strided by 544 floats: same issue. But smem is much faster than L2 cache. So keeping c outer (minimizing smem stride) might be better? Wait, no. With c outer, the inner loop over co is contiguous in weight (good) and the c loop is strided in smem. With kd,kh,kw outer, the c loop would be inner, which is strided in weight. Actually, in the original code, for each c, we do 27 smem loads (one per kd,kh,kw) and 27 * 24 weight loads. Since smem is fast, the cost of 48 strided smem loads is minor. The weight loads dominate, and they're contiguous for fixed c. So original order is good. One more idea: can I store the smem in [d][h][w][c] order instead? Then for fixed d,h,w and varying c, access is contiguous. Layout: [d_s][h_s][w_s][c] Address = ((d_s * H + h_s) * W + w_s) * C_in + c For fixed d_s,h_s,w_s and varying c: addresses are base, base+1, ..., base+C_in-1. Contiguous! For the compute loop with c outer: we'd access base_c = ((d_s*H+h_s)*W+w_s)*C_in + c for each c. This is still strided by D*H*W*C_in floats between consecutive c values in the outer loop. Wait, in the original loop: ```cuda for c in 0..47: for kd,kh,kw: inp = smem[((c * D + d_s) * H + h_s) * W + w_s] ``` For layout [c][d][h][w], consecutive c values are separated by D*H*W floats. For layout [d][h][w][c], consecutive c values for the SAME spatial position are contiguous. BUT we're changing c in the OUTER loop, and for EACH c, we access different (d_s,h_s,w_s). So the address for iteration (c, kd, kh, kw) is: Layout [d][h][w][c]: addr = ((d_s*H+h_s)*W+w_s)*C_in + c As c increments by 1, addr increments by 1. But d_s, h_s, w_s also change with kd,kh,kw. For fixed c, varying kd,kh,kw: d_s = d_local + kd. So for (kd=0,kh=0,kw=0): addr0 = ((d0*H+h0)*W+w0)*C_in + c. For (kd=0,kh=0,kw=1): addr1 = ((d0*H+h0)*W+w0+1)*C_in + c = addr0 + C_in. For (kd=0,kh=1,kw=0): addr2 = ((d0*H+h0+1)*W+w0)*C_in + c = addr0 + W*C_in. So within a c iteration, the smem accesses are strided by C_in or W*C_in. With C_in=48, W=34: W*C_in = 1632 floats = 6528 bytes. This is a large stride. For layout [c][d][h][w]: addr = (c*D*H*W + d_s*H*W + h_s*W + w_s). Within a c iteration: consecutive kw differ by 1 (contiguous). Consecutive kh differ by W. Consecutive kd differ by H*W. Between c iterations: difference is D*H*W. So layout [c][d][h][w] gives contiguous access for kw loop, strided by W for kh, and strided by H*W for kd. Since W=34 float = 136 bytes (multiple of 4 but not 128), the kh step might cause some bank conflicts but not terrible. Layout [d][h][w][c] gives strided by C_in=48 floats=192 bytes for kw. 192 bytes is 1.5 cache lines, which might be okay but not great. And strided by W*C_in for kh. For our access pattern where we loop over kd,kh,kw for each c, and we want good spatial locality WITHIN the inner kd,kh,kw loops, layout [c][d][h][w] is better because varying w_s gives contiguous access. So I'll stick with [c][d][h][w]. Actually, for bank conflict analysis with [c][d][h][w]: For threads in a warp accessing different w_s but same c,d_s,h_s: Addresses: base + w_s. Contiguous. Banks: base%32, (base+1)%32, ... for up to 32 threads. No conflict if all w_s are distinct mod 32. In our block (D=2,H=2,W=32, block=128): - Warp 0 (t=0..31): d=0,h=0,w=0..31. w_s = w_local + kw = 0..31 + kw. Banks = (base + w_s) % 32 = all distinct. No conflict! - Warp 1 (t=32..63): d=0,h=1,w=0..31. w_s = 0..31 + kw. Base' = base + H*W = base + 4*34 = base + 136. Banks = (base + 136 + w_s) % 32 = (base + 8 + w_s) % 32 (since 136 % 32 = 8). For w_s = 0..31: banks = base+8, base+9, ..., base+39=base+7 mod 32. Distinct! No conflict. - Warp 2 (t=64..95): d=1,h=0,w=0..31. Base'' = base + D*H*W*C_in? Wait, no. Address = c*D*H*W + d_s*H*W + h_s*W + w_s. For warp 2: d_s=1, h_s=0. Base'' = c*D*H*W + 1*H*W + 0*W = base + H*W = base + 4*34 = base + 136. Same as warp 1! But in a different warp, so no bank conflict within a warp. Wait, warp 2 (t=64..95) has h_local = (64/32)%2 = 0? Let me recalculate. t=64..95: w_local = t%32 = 0..31. h_local = (t/32)%2 = 2%2 = 0. d_local = t/64 = 1. So warp 2: d=1, h=0. Address = c*D*H*W + 1*H*W + 0 + w_s = base + 4*34 + w_s = base + 136 + w_s. Warp 0: d=0,h=0. Address = base + w_s. No conflict within each warp. So with [c][d][h][w] layout and W_TILE=32, we have genuinely zero bank conflicts within each warp. This is great! And weight access: for each warp, all threads access the same weight sequence. Good for L2. OK, I'm now fairly confident in this design: - D_TILE=2, H_TILE=2, W_TILE=32, BLOCK_SIZE=128 - Layout [c][d_s][h_s][w_s] - Input in shared memory only - Weight from global/L2 - K=3 hardcoded and unrolled Let me write the final code. One last optimization: for the output write, 128 threads each writing 24 channels. Instead of each thread writing channels sequentially (scattered writes in [n][c][d][h][w]), can we reorder or use vectorized writes? Output layout is [n][c][d][h][w]. Each thread writes: output[n, 0, d, h, w], output[n, 1, d, h, w], ..., output[n, 23, d, h, w]. These are separated by D*H*W = 98*98*98 = 941,192 floats = 3.76 MB. Very far apart! Not coalesced at all. But wait, we already established that within a warp, threads have the same d,h but different w. So for channel co=0: Thread t writes to output[n, 0, d, h, w_t]. These are CONTIGUOUS across the warp! Because w varies. Then for co=1: thread t writes to output[n, 1, d, h, w_t]. Also contiguous across the warp. So each channel writes separately, and within each channel, the warp writes coalesced. This is the best we can do with NCHW format. It's actually quite good: 24 coalesced writes per thread. Can we use float4 for the output? No, because we're writing 1 float per thread per channel. float4 would require 4 consecutive channels with same n,d,h,w but... wait, for a fixed thread, it writes channels 0,1,2,3 sequentially, which are strided by D*H*W. Not contiguous. So scalar writes are the way to go. Now, what about using a different output layout? If we use NHWC or something, maybe writes are faster. But PyTorch uses NCHW for Conv3d, and the output must match. Could we write to a temporary buffer in NHWC format and then transpose? That adds overhead. I'll stick with NCHW and accept the non-coalesced cross-channel writes. Since there are only 24 channels, and each channel has a coalesced write, the overall write bandwidth should be fine. One more concern: the total output is 722 MB. Writing it 24 times (once per channel sequentially) means we need 722 MB of write bandwidth. At 3.35 TB/s, that's ~0.2 ms. Negligible compared to total compute. OK, I'm ready to write the final code. Let me draft it carefully: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> // Fast kernel for K=3, stride=1, pad=0, dilation=1, groups=1 __global__ void conv_transpose3d_fast_k3( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out ) { const int K = 3; const int D_TILE = 2; const int H_TILE = 2; const int W_TILE = 32; const int BLOCK_SIZE = D_TILE * H_TILE * W_TILE; // 128 int n = blockIdx.z; int w_base = blockIdx.x * W_TILE; int spatial_idx = blockIdx.y; int num_h_tiles = (H_out + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE; int h_idx = spatial_idx % num_h_tiles; int d_idx = spatial_idx / num_h_tiles; int h_base = h_idx * H_TILE; int d_base = d_idx * D_TILE; const int D_SMEM = D_TILE + K - 1; // 4 const int H_SMEM = H_TILE + K - 1; // 4 const int W_SMEM = W_TILE + K - 1; // 34 const int SMEM_VOLUME = D_SMEM * H_SMEM * W_SMEM; // 544 extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Cooperative load: layout [c][d_s][h_s][w_s] int total_load = C_in * SMEM_VOLUME; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_load; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { int w_s = idx % W_SMEM; int tmp = idx / W_SMEM; int h_s = tmp % H_SMEM; int d_s = (tmp / H_SMEM) % D_SMEM; int c = tmp / (H_SMEM * D_SMEM); int d_in = d_base + d_s - (K - 1); int h_in = h_base + h_s - (K - 1); int w_in = w_base + w_s - (K - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (d_in >= 0 && d_in < D_in && h_in >= 0 && h_in < H_in && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + c) * D_in + d_in) * H_in * W_in + h_in * W_in + w_in]; } smem[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); int tid = threadIdx.x; int w_local = tid % W_TILE; int h_local = (tid / W_TILE) % H_TILE; int d_local = tid / (W_TILE * H_TILE); int w_out = w_base + w_local; int h_out = h_base + h_local; int d_out = d_base + d_local; if (d_out >= D_out || h_out >= H_out || w_out >= W_out) return; const int MAX_CO = 32; float acc[MAX_CO]; if (bias) { for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) acc[co] = bias[co]; } else { for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) acc[co] = 0.0f; } for (int c = 0; c < C_in; c++) { #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < K; kd++) { int d_s = d_local + kd; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { int h_s = h_local + kh; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int w_s = w_local + kw; float inp = smem[((c * D_SMEM + d_s) * H_SMEM + h_s) * W_SMEM + w_s]; int w_idx_base = ((c * C_out) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw; for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { acc[co] += inp * weight[w_idx_base + co]; } } } } } int out_base = ((n * C_out) * D_out + d_out) * H_out * W_out + h_out * W_out + w_out; int c_stride = D_out * H_out * W_out; for (int co = 0; co < C_out; co++) { output[out_base + co * c_stride] = acc[co]; } } // General fallback kernel __global__ void conv_transpose3d_general( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_d, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_d, int dil_h, int dil_w, int groups, bool has_bias ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w_out = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h_out = tmp % H_out; tmp /= H_out; int d_out = tmp % D_out; int c_out = tmp / D_out; int n = c_out / C_out; c_out = c_out % C_out; int C_out_per_group = C_out / groups; int C_in_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / C_out_per_group; int c_out_g = c_out % C_out_per_group; float val = has_bias ? bias[c_out] : 0.0f; for (int c_in_g = 0; c_in_g < C_in_per_group; c_in_g++) { int c_in = g * C_in_per_group + c_in_g; for (int kd = 0; kd < K; kd++) { int d_in_numer = d_out + pad_d - kd * dil_d; if (d_in_numer < 0 || d_in_numer % stride_d != 0) continue; int d_in = d_in_numer / stride_d; if (d_in >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { int h_in_numer = h_out + pad_h - kh * dil_h; if (h_in_numer < 0 || h_in_numer % stride_h != 0) continue; int h_in = h_in_numer / stride_h; if (h_in >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int w_in_numer = w_out + pad_w - kw * dil_w; if (w_in_numer < 0 || w_in_numer % stride_w != 0) continue; int w_in = w_in_numer / stride_w; if (w_in >= W_in) continue; float in_val = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * D_in + d_in) * H_in * W_in + h_in * W_in + w_in]; float w_val = weight[((c_in * C_out_per_group + c_out_g) * K + kd) * K * K + kh * K + kw]; val += in_val * w_val; } } } } output[((n * C_out + c_out) * D_out + d_out) * H_out * W_out + h_out * W_out + w_out] = val; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_d, int pad_h, int pad_w, int out_pad_d, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int dil_d, int dil_h, int dil_w, int groups ) { auto N = input.size(0); auto C_in = input.size(1); auto D_in = input.size(2); auto H_in = input.size(3); auto W_in = input.size(4); auto C_out_per_group = weight.size(1); auto K = weight.size(2); auto C_out = C_out_per_group * groups; auto D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride_d - 2 * pad_d + dil_d * (K - 1) + out_pad_d + 1; auto H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride_h - 2 * pad_h + dil_h * (K - 1) + out_pad_h + 1; auto W_out = (W_in - 1) * stride_w - 2 * pad_w + dil_w * (K - 1) + out_pad_w + 1; auto output = torch::empty({N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); bool has_bias = bias.has_value(); if (stride_d == 1 && stride_h == 1 && stride_w == 1 && pad_d == 0 && pad_h == 0 && pad_w == 0 && out_pad_d == 0 && out_pad_h == 0 && out_pad_w == 0 && dil_d == 1 && dil_h == 1 && dil_w == 1 && groups == 1) { const int D_TILE = 2; const int H_TILE = 2; const int W_TILE = 32; const int BLOCK_SIZE = D_TILE * H_TILE * W_TILE; dim3 grid( (W_out + W_TILE - 1) / W_TILE, ((H_out + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE) * ((D_out + D_TILE - 1) / D_TILE), N ); int D_SMEM = D_TILE + K - 1; int H_SMEM = H_TILE + K - 1; int W_SMEM = W_TILE + K - 1; size_t smem_size = C_in * D_SMEM * H_SMEM * W_SMEM * sizeof(float); if (K == 3) { if (smem_size > 48 * 1024) { cudaFuncSetAttribute(conv_transpose3d_fast_k3, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, smem_size); } conv_transpose3d_fast_k3<<<grid, BLOCK_SIZE, smem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), has_bias ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out ); } else { // For K != 3 with other params as fast path, use general kernel for now int total = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; int threads = 256; int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; conv_transpose3d_general<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), has_bias ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out, K, stride_d, stride_h, stride_w, pad_d, pad_h, pad_w, dil_d, dil_h, dil_w, groups, has_bias ); } } else { int total = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; int threads = 256; int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; conv_transpose3d_general<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), has_bias ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out, K, stride_d, stride_h, stride_w, pad_d, pad_h, pad_w, dil_d, dil_h, dil_w, groups, has_bias ); } return output; } """ cpp_source = "" # No C++ wrappers needed; load_inline handles it module = load_inline( name='conv_transpose3d_cuda', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['conv_transpose3d_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=True, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels=48, out_channels=24, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, dilation=1, groups=1, bias=False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.stride = stride if isinstance(stride, tuple) else (stride, stride, stride) self.padding = padding if isinstance(padding, tuple) else (padding, padding, padding) self.output_padding = output_padding if isinstance(output_padding, tuple) else (output_padding, output_padding, output_padding) self.dilation = dilation if isinstance(dilation, tuple) else (dilation, dilation, dilation) self.groups = groups self.has_bias = bias self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(in_channels, out_channels // groups, kernel_size, kernel_size, kernel_size)) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if self.bias is not None: fan_in, _ = nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) nn.init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) def forward(self, x): return module.conv_transpose3d_cuda( x, self.weight, self.bias if self.has_bias else None, self.stride[0], self.stride[1], self.stride[2], self.padding[0], self.padding[1], self.padding[2], self.output_padding[0], self.output_padding[1], self.output_padding[2], self.dilation[0], self.dilation[1], self.dilation[2], self.groups ) ``` Wait, there's a problem with the init. `nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight)` expects Conv weight layout [out_c, in_c, ...]. Our weight is [in_c, out_c, ...]. So fan_in would be out_c * k^3 and fan_out would be in_c. That's reversed from what PyTorch uses. For ConvTranspose3d, the weight is stored as [in_c, out_c//groups, K, K, K]. PyTorch's init for ConvTranspose3d calculates fan_in = in_channels * k^3 and fan_out = out_channels. Let me check: `nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out` looks at dimensions [0] and [1] as out_c and in_c. For our weight, dim[0]=in_c, dim[1]=out_c//groups. So it would compute: fan_in = out_c//groups * k^3 fan_out = in_c But PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d init might manually swap these, or might use the transpose weight's dimensions directly. Actually, PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d uses the same _init_weights as Conv3d: ```python init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if self.bias is not None: fan_in, _ = init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) ``` For ConvTranspose3d weight shape (in_c, out_c, k, k, k), `_calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out` computes: fan_in = out_c * k^3 (treats dim[1] as in_channels for a conv layer) fan_out = in_c * k^3 (treats dim[0] as out_channels) Wait, no. Looking at PyTorch source: ```python def _calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(tensor): dimensions = tensor.dim() if dimensions < 2: raise ValueError(...) num_input_fmaps = tensor.size(1) num_output_fmaps = tensor.size(0) receptive_field_size = 1 if tensor.dim() > 2: for s in tensor.shape[2:]: receptive_field_size *= s fan_in = num_input_fmaps * receptive_field_size fan_out = num_output_fmaps * receptive_field_size return fan_in, fan_out ``` So for weight [in_c, out_c, k, k, k]: num_input_fmaps = out_c num_output_fmaps = in_c fan_in = out_c * k^3 fan_out = in_c * k^3 This is SWAPPED compared to what you'd expect for a transpose conv. But typically in benchmarks, `x` is moved to CUDA before being passed. Let me not worry about this. Actually, I'll leave `x` as-is and not call `.contiguous()` explicitly in Python. Instead, in the C++ wrapper, I can call `.contiguous()` before passing to the kernel. ```cuda torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_cuda(torch::Tensor input, ...) { input = input.contiguous(); weight = weight.contiguous(); if (bias.has_value()) bias.value() = bias.value().contiguous(); // ... rest } ``` Actually, `c10::optional<torch::Tensor>` doesn't support reassigning the optional. Better just ensure in Python: ```python def forward(self, x): x = x.contiguous() w = self.conv_transpose3d.weight.contiguous() b = self.conv_transpose3d.bias.contiguous() if self.conv_transpose3d.bias is not None else None return module.conv_transpose3d_cuda(x, w, b, ...) ``` Or even simpler, just call `.contiguous()` on everything before passing. Actually, parameters are typically always contiguous. Input might or might not be. I'll do `x = x.contiguous()` in Python forward. One more thought: if `w` is a Parameter, calling `w.contiguous()` returns a Tensor (not a Parameter). This is fine for our CUDA kernel which expects `torch::Tensor`. Let me now write the final, complete Python code.
